PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Criminal Court, Hamblen County (Tennessee), which convicted him of first-degree felony murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: A grocery store clerk was murdered during a robbery. Defendant was a coworker and was seen both near the store at the time of the murder and driving away at a high rate of speed. The court found that the jury venire was proper. The court found that the sentence was improper because use of the aggravating circumstance in Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-203(i)(7) to impose a death sentence in cases of felony murder violated the Eighth Amendment and Tenn. Const. art. I, § 16.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for felony murder, reversed and set aside the sentence of death, and remanded the cause to the trial court for further appropriate proceedings.

 

125 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Middlebrooks


No. 01-S-01-9102-CR-00008


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


840 S.W.2d 317; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 563



September 8, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Criminal Court. Davidson County. HON. ANN LACY JOHNS, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTION AFFIRMED; SENTENCE REVERSED AND REMANDED FOR RE-SENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the trial court (Tennessee), which convicted him of first degree felony murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree felony murder, and he was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court improperly overruled his motion to suppress his taped confession because he did not knowingly and voluntarily waive his rights. The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant, ruling that although the right to counsel and the right against self-incrimination were constitutional rights, they could be waived provided the waiver was made voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently. The court found that in determining whether a confession was made knowingly and voluntarily, courts looked to the totality of the circumstances and that, before there could be a voluntary and knowing waiver, the accused must be adequately and effectively apprised of his rights. The court concluded that defendant knowingly and voluntarily waived his constitutional rights and voluntarily confessed his involvement in the murder under the totality of the circumstances. The court remanded the case for resentencing, however, holding that it was unable to conclude that the elimination of an aggravating circumstance was harmless error beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendant was affirmed, but the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

126 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hale


S/C No. 48


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


840 S.W.2d 307; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 507



August 3, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition to Rehear Denied September 8, 1992, Reported at 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 558.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CRIMINAL COURT. WASHINGTON COUNTY. HON. LYNN W. BROWN, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Criminal Court, Washington County (Tennessee) for first degree murder under The Scotty Trexler Law, Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-202(a)(2) (Supp. 1988) for the beating death of a two-year old boy.


OVERVIEW: The two-year old boy had been beaten by defendant and died from a very deep tear in the liver and a tear in the small bowel mesentery. The court reversed the conviction finding that § 39-2-2-202(a)(2) required the prosecution to establish prior instances of misdemeanor child abuse to prove the elements of first-degree murder. This requirement was fundamentally unfair and deprived defendant of due process in contravention the "law of the land" provision of Tenn. Const. art. I, § 8. Defendant had not been convicted of any instances of abuse of the victim. The court also reversed the death sentence, finding that it was disproportionate to the crime on which the jury was charged. The defendant became subject to execution solely on the basis of the commission of a misdemeanor. Because other killings that resulted from the commissions of misdemeanors were classified only as manslaughter, death eligibility under the provisions of Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-202(a)(2) (Supp. 1988) was constitutionally disproportionate punishment violative of Tenn. Const. art. I, § 16.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder in the beating death of a two-year old boy.

Knox County ex rel. Kessel v. Lenoir City


S/C No. 16


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


837 S.W.2d 382; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 505



July 27, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CHANCERY COURT. LOUDON COUNTY. Hon. Frank V. Williams, III, Judge



DISPOSITION: TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a decision of the Chancery Court of Loudon County (Tennessee) which held that Chapter 205 of the Private Acts of 1970 (Chapter 205) governing distribution of tax-equivalent payments on property owned by appellee city's utilities board (board) was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The county brought an action against the city to declare unconstitutional Chapter 205, which governed distribution of tax equivalent payments on property owned by the city's utilities board. The county contended that Chapter 205 conflicted with a provision of the current tax equivalents statute, Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 7-52-301--310, which had mandatory, statewide application and that for state constitutional purposes the statute was a general law. Thus Chapter 205, which affected only the distribution of payments to the city, was unconstitutional under the Tn. Const. art. XI, § 8, because the payments were not distributed in compliance with the general law. The trial court held that Chapter 205 was constitutional and the county appealed. The court affirmed, ruling that §§ 7-52-301--310, specifically allowed the allocation of payments among taxing jurisdictions to be established by means of a private act, such as Chapter 205. Thus Chapter 205 was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which found that the Private Acts of 1970, which governed the distribution of local tax payments on property owned by the city, did not conflict with the current tax equivalents statute and was therefore constitutional.

Potts v. State


02-S-01-9201-CR-00001


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT JACKSON


833 S.W.2d 60; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 424



June 8, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 22, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Criminal Court. Shelby County. No. 90-11396 Below. Hon. Joseph B. Dailey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought review of an order of the Criminal Court of Shelby County (Tennessee), which granted the motion of the State and dismissed the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief on the basis that it was time barred by the three-year statute of limitations of Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-102.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was indicted for a third offense DUI. He pled guilty, but he contested his third time offender status. The inmate filed a motion styled as a petition for post-conviction relief, which challenged the validity of his two prior guilty pleas seven and eight years before. The State moved to dismiss the post-conviction action on the basis that it was time barred. The trial court granted the State's motion and convicted the inmate. The inmate appealed on the basis that § 40-30-102 was a suspension of the writ of habeas corpus in violation of Tenn. Const. art. I, § 15. The court held, in affirming, that the limitations period was not applicable to habeas corpus because that was a different avenue of collateral attack separate from post-conviction relief. Post-conviction actions applied to convictions that were allegedly void or voidable because of an abridgement of constitutional rights and were filed under Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-105. Habeas corpus actions were restricted to cases of a void judgment or to free an inmate held after the term of imprisonment expired, were filed under Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 29-21-101 et seq., and were not subject to any statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief.

First Utility Dist. v. Clark


No. 03-S-01-9109-CH-00087


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


834 S.W.2d 283; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 364



May 26, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] EQUITY COURT. CARTER COUNTY. HON. RICHARD JOHNSON, CHANCELLOR



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff utility district sought review of a decision of the Equity Court, Carter County (Tennessee), which entered judgment for defendant county executive, under Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 9, in the utility district's action seeking a declaration that an amendment to the 1937 Utility District Act, Tenn. Code Ann. § 7-82-307 (1980, 1985, Supp. 1991) violated Tenn. Const. art. I, § 20, art. II, § 3, art. XI, § 8, and U.S. Const. art. I, § 10.


OVERVIEW: Tenn. Code Ann. § 7-82-307(aa) (Supp. 1991), allowed the legislative body of the county to change the manner of filling vacancies on the utility district's board from selection by the incumbent commissioners and county judge to a method of election by the utility district customers. The utility district contended on appeal that § 7-82-307(aa) unconstitutionally delegated legislative power in violation of Tenn. Const. art. II, § 3 and impaired the obligations of contract under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, Tenn. Const. art. I, § 20, and that it suspended a general law for the benefit of particular individuals and altered corporate powers in violation of Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 8. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that under Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 9, Tenn. Code Ann. § 7-82-307(aa) (Supp. 1991) was complete when it left the hands of the legislature. The court held that the legislature did not unlawfully delegate its authority, and that § 7-82-307(aa) did not unconstitutionally impair the utility district's obligations of contract, suspend a general law for the benefit of particular individuals, or alter the utility district's corporate powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court for the county executive, finding that a statutory amendment did not violate the state or federal constitution, in the utility district's action seeking a declaratory judgment that the amendment was unconstitutional.

State v. Harris


S/C No. 36


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


839 S.W.2d 54; 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 348



May 11, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing September 8, 1992, Reported at 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 557.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] CRIMINAL COURT. SEVIER COUNTY. Hon. J. Kenneth Porter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Criminal Court, Sevier County (Tennessee), which convicted him of murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, and he was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred by excusing for cause potential jurors who expressed concern about imposing death as a penalty, without providing defendant with an opportunity to rehabilitate each juror. The court affirmed the judgment convicting and sentencing defendant, ruling that the responses of the prospective jurors revealed that their views on the death penalty would prevent or substantially impair the performance of their duties as jurors in accordance with their instructions and their oaths, and the trial court's finding on the issue was accorded a presumption of correctness inasmuch as such findings involved a determination of demeanor and credibility particularly within the trial court's province. The court concluded that the burden rested on defendant to establish by convincing evidence that the trial court's determination was erroneous and that defendant failed to meet the burden.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant and sentencing him to death was affirmed.

Civil Service Merit Bd. v. Burson


No. 319


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Knoxville


816 S.W.2d 725; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 386



September 16, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Knox County, The Hon. Frederick McDonald Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Knoxville Civil Service Merit Board and its members, as well as a civic group (members), sought a declaration that Tenn. Code Ann. § 6-54-114 violated guarantees of local control found in Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 9 as well as federal and state constitution equal protection guarantees. After the Knox County Court (Tennessee) upheld the statute and ruled in favor of appellee city, local, and state officials, the members appealed.


OVERVIEW: The statute upheld by the trial court sought to make uniform the qualifications and procedures applied to municipal civil service boards in the state's most populous counties. Because their city had adopted home rule pursuant to Tenn. Const. art. IX, § 9, the members claimed that the statute violated home rule and equal protection. The court affirmed. It found that because the statute did not impermissibly interfere with "local control of local affairs," no home rule violation resulted. The court found that Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 8 did not apply and did not review the statute thereunder. The court also found that the statutory classifications passed constitutional muster under Tenn. Const. art. I, § 8 because they were neither unreasonable nor unfair and because a legitimate justification for the classifications could be envisioned. Rejecting claims of equal protection violations resulting from the residency requirements, the court refused to apply a strict scrutiny analysis, ruling that because the requirement did not "penalize" any underlying fundamental right, it was properly upheld based on its rational relationship to a legitimate state objective.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the declaratory judgment entered by the trial court finding that the challenged statute was constitutional.

 

132 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


PLANNED PARENTHOOD ASSN. OF NASHVILLE, INC. v. NcWHERTER


No. 01-S-01-9101-01-0004


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


817 S.W.2d 13; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 347



September 9, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 14, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 428.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certified Question from the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter was before the court on a certified question referred from the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit. The court of appeals asked the court to render an opinion on the relative validity of two statutes, Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 37-10-301 et seq. (1988), that required parental consent for abortions by minors, and Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-15-202(f) (1989), that required notice to parents or guardians or minors seeking an abortion.


OVERVIEW: In 1988, the general assembly enacted Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 37-10-301 et seq. (1988), which mandated parental consent for abortions by minors. In 1989, the general assembly enacted Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-15-202(f) (1989), which mandated notice to parents or guardians or minors seeking an abortion. The matter came before the the court by way of a certified question of law referred from the U.S. court of appeals. The court held that because of an irreconcilable conflict between the provisions of the 1988 parental consent statute, Tenn. Code Ann. § 37-10-303, and those of the 1989 parental notification statute, Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-15-202(f), Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-15-202(f) effectively repealed Tenn. Code Ann. § 37-10-303 by implication. The court held that under principles of statutory construction and constitutional interpretation, the provisions of Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-15-202(f) should have been considered valid, unless they were successfully challenged before a court having jurisdiction to rule on their validity. The court would not issue an advisory opinion on the constitutionality of either statute.


OUTCOME: The court held that the 1989 statute repealed the 1988 statute. The court declined to deliver an advisory opinion on the constitutionality of either statute. The court directed the clerk to submit that opinion to the court of appeals.

L.L. Bean, Inc. v. Bracey


No. 01S01-9010-CH-00087


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


817 S.W.2d 292; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 346



September 9, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Davidson County, The Hon. C. Allen High, Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: TRIAL COURT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a ruling from the Chancery Court of Davidson County (Tennessee), which determined that it did not have jurisdiction to entertain the taxpayer's constitutional challenge to Tennessee's sales tax statute, Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-6-102, against defendants, the attorney general, the Tennessee Commissioner of Revenue, and the sales tax director (commissioner).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer, a Maine corporation, filed an action challenging the constitutionality of Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-6-102(6)(J), which imposed sales tax on catalog sales by "dealers" to Tennessee consumers. The trial court granted the commissioner's motion to dismiss for lack of subject matter jurisdiction. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-1-1801 et seq. provided the exclusive remedy for challenging any liability the taxpayer had for Tennessee sales taxes and that the taxpayer could raise its constitutional objections in such action. The court rejected the taxpayer's reliance on 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 to impose jurisdiction, finding that the Tax Injunction Act of 1937, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1341, which limited federal jurisdiction, applied to state court actions as well as where the taxpayer had been afforded a plain, speedy, and efficient statutory remedy. The court also found that Tenn. Code Ann. § 4-5-224 did not provide jurisdiction because the language "unless otherwise specifically provided by statute" encompassed § 67-1-804, which provided exclusive jurisdiction for determining liability for taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the taxpayer's complaint, which had challenged the constitutionality of the sales tax statute, due to lack of subject matter jurisdiction.

Metropolitan Government of Nashville & Davidson County v. Tennessee State Bd. of Equalization


Appeal No. 01-S-01-9007-CH-00062


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


817 S.W.2d 953; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 342



September 3, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied October 28, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 433.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Chancery Court, Davidson Court, Hon. Irvin H. Kilcrease, Jr., Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED & REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant metropolitan government (metropolitan) field a declaratory judgment action in the Chancery Court, Davidson Court (Tennessee) against appellee Tennessee State Board of Equalization (board) challenging the constitutionality of Tenn. Code Ann. §67-5-212(a)(1)(1984). The trial court denied the metropolitan's request. The metropolitan took direct appeal to the court under Tenn. Code Ann. § 16-4-108.


OVERVIEW:  Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-5-212(a)(1)(1984) exempted from taxation real and personal property used exclusively for religious purposes. The 1984 amendment to the statute stated that a church parsonage was deemed property used purely and exclusively for religious purposes. Tenn. Const. art. II, § 28 (1972) allowed the state to exempt from taxation property used purely for religious purposes. The court noted that the use requirement was met where the use was incidental to or an integral part of one of the recognized purposes of an exempt institution. The court held that the 1984 amendment to the statute was unconstitutional under § 28. The court found that the amendment sought to exempt all church parsonages per se without regard to whether the parsonages were in fact used purely and exclusively for the required purposes, in excess of the authority granted the legislature under § 28.


OUTCOME: The court, in the metropolitan's declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of a statute exempting from taxation church parsonages, reversed the ruling of the trial court upholding the act's constitutionality. The court remanded for any necessary proceedings.

State v. Black


S/C No. 01-S-01-9002-CR-00007


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


815 S.W.2d 166; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 322



August 5, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Overruled September 3, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 344.
Rehearing overruled by State v. Black, 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 344 (Tenn., Sept. 3, 1991)
Post-conviction relief denied at Black v. State, 2005 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 1129 (Tenn. Crim. App., Oct. 19, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: From the Criminal Court, Davidson County, Hon. Walter C. Kurtz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment entered by the Criminal Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), of conviction of defendant for three murders and also sought review of the death sentence imposed upon one of the convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the triple murders of his girlfriend and her two young daughters. Defendant received life sentences for two of the murders and a death sentence for one of the murders. On appeal, the court affirmed. It upheld the constitutionality of the Death Penalty Statute, Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-203. It was not invalid per se as cruel and unusual punishment under Tenn. Const. art. I, § 16 or under U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court also held that the trial court did not err in ruling that defendant was competent to stand trial. The evidence was sufficient to support the convictions beyond a reasonable doubt. The trial court did not err in excluding evidence that electrocution was a cruel and unusual punishment. The three murders met the mass murder statutory aggravating circumstance of Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-203(i)(12). The statute encompassed a series of separate but related murders committed as part of a common scheme or plan. The death sentence was neither excessive nor disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first degree murder convictions and the death sentence imposed by the trial court upon defendant. The evidence was sufficient. Triple murders committed as part of a common scheme or plan met the aggravating circumstance of mass murder. The death penalty statute was constitutional. The death sentence was not disproportionate or excessive. The court set the date for the death sentence to be carried out.

Sawyers v. State


No. 34


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


814 S.W.2d 725; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 514



July 29, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Sawyers v. State, 1997 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 704 (Tenn. Crim. App., July 25, 1997)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Hamblen County. No. 88-CR-228. The Hon. William H. Inman, Special Judge
Sawyers v. State, 1990 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 24 (Tenn. Crim. App., Jan. 16, 1990)



DISPOSITION: Trial Court Reversed; Court of Criminal Appeals Reversed; Remanded


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed an order from the Court of Appeals (Tennessee), which, in the inmate's petition for post-conviction relief, affirmed an order of the trial court that denied the inmate's request.


OVERVIEW: The inmate, ignorant of his actual birth date, was mistakenly indicted as an adult. He entered guilty pleas to first degree murder, armed robbery, and grand larceny. The issue was whether relief could be granted to an inmate who was actually a juvenile at the time of the offense but who was not afforded a transfer hearing in juvenile court prior to his conviction in criminal court, because neither he nor the State knew that he was underage. The court held that the absence of a transfer order did not affect the criminal court's subject matter jurisdiction, which was concurrent with that of the juvenile court as to certain offenses committed by children within a specified age span. The only requirement was that such proceedings had to originate in juvenile court. The absence of a juvenile transfer order was a procedural deficiency that was sufficiently fundamental to be considered a matter of due process in the context of juvenile justice. The court returned the case for a de novo hearing. If the inmate would have been transferred to criminal court, the conviction would stand; if not, it would be vacated and the prosecution recommenced in juvenile court, as required by statute.


OUTCOME: The court returned the case to the trial court for a de novo hearing to determine whether or not the inmate would have been transferred from juvenile to criminal court, based on the facts existing at the time of his indictment and trial. If a transfer would have been appropriate, the inmate's conviction would be permitted to stand. If not, it had to be vacated and the prosecution recommenced in juvenile court, as required by statute.

Lowe'S Companies, Inc. v. Cardwell


Appeal No. 01-S-01-9004 CH-00032


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review of an order from the Chancery Court for Davidson County (Tennessee), which held in favor of appellee Tennessee Commissioner of Revenue in the company's action seeking a refund of sales and use taxes assessed under Tenn. OVERVIEW: The company purchased from out-of-state companies preprinted advertising supplements that was distributed through Tennessee newspapers. Pursuant to Tenn. State v. Bobo


App. No. 01-S-01-9005-CR-00040


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


814 S.W.2d 353; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 291



July 8, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Davidson County, Hon. J. Randall Wyatt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Co-defendants sought review of an affirmance by the Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee) of a judgment of conviction of second degree murder in the trial court. Co-defendants sought a determination of whether the trial judge committed reversible error by replacing one of the regular jurors with a previously discharged alternative after deliberations had begun.


OVERVIEW: A juror speculated during deliberations that a co-defendant killed her cousin. She was excused and an alternate recalled. The alternate was reseated and the jury returned to deliberations without being recharged or instructed to begin deliberations anew. The appellate court found that while the action was error, it was harmless. The court held that the substitution of jurors after final submission of the case, coupled with the trial court's failure to instruct the jury to begin deliberations anew, violated each co-defendant's right to a trial by jury under Tenn. Const. art. I, § 6 as it was unclear that the alternate juror took part in all the deliberations. The court also rejected the appeals court's view that the error was harmless in view of the overwhelming evidence of defendants' guilt. The court observed that any errors affecting the constitutional right to trial by jury resulted in such prejudice to the judicial process that automatic reversal was required as such violations were defects in the structure of the trial mechanism and thus defied analysis by harmless error standards. The court found no voluntary relinquishment of the right to be tried by a common law jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' judgment of conviction of second degree murder in the trial court and remanded the cause.

141 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Southern R. Co. v. Taylor


No. 01-S-01-9002-CH-00019


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


812 S.W.2d 577; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 250



June 10, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal from the Equity Court, Davidson County, Hon. Irvin H. Kilcrease, Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF CHANCERY COURT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the judgment from the Equity Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), which held in favor of appellees, the Commissioner of Revenue, State of Tennessee, and the Department of Revenue of the State of Tennessee, in the taxpayer's action to recover corporate excise taxes paid. The Commissioner disallowed a net operating loss carryover for purposes of the Tennessee Excise Tax Law, Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-4-801 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer sought the recovery of taxes, interest, and penalties paid pursuant to the Commissioner's assessment. The Tennessee Excise Tax Law, Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-4-801 et seq., imposed a tax on corporations doing business in Tennessee. The tax was subject to certain adjustments, including the subtraction of dividends earned by a parent corporation from a subsidiary corporation where the parent owned 80 percent or more of the subsidiary's stock. The Commissioner disallowed the carryover pursuant to its Rev. Rul. 1320-6-1-.21. The court reversed. The deduction for dividends received from 80 percent subsidiaries was an allowable deduction in computing net earnings for Tennessee corporate excise tax purposes and allowable in computing a net operating loss pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-4-805(b)(2)(C). The Commissioner's Rev. Rul. 1320-6-1-.21 calling for such dividends to be added to a net loss as determined for excise tax purposes had the effect of disallowing the deduction for such dividends for purposes of Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-4-805(b)(2)(C). The Commissioner's Rev. Rul. 1320-6-1-.21 was inconsistent and void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment in favor of the Commissioner that disallowed the taxpayer's net operating loss carryover and granted the taxpayer a refund plus interest. The court remanded for further necessary proceedings, including the determination of the amount of attorneys' fees and expenses of litigation to be awarded to the taxpayer.

 

142 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Itel Containers Int'l Corp. v. Cardwell


S/C No. 01-S-01-9005-CH-00038


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


814 S.W.2d 29; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 169; 1992 AMC 2540



April 22, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Chancery Court, Davidson County, Hon. Robert Brandt, Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged the judgment of the Chancery Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), which found in favor of appellee, the Tennessee Commissioner of Revenue (commissioner), in the corporation's action challenging the imposition of the sales tax on the transfer of its cargo containers, pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-6-201.


OVERVIEW: The corporation instituted an action to recover monies paid, under protest, for the sales tax assessed on the transfer of its cargo containers. The trial court upheld the tax assessment and the corporation appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the imposition of a sales tax upon the transfer of possession in Tennessee of domestically-owned cargo containers used exclusively in international commerce was constitutionally permissible. Because the sales tax was imposed upon the discrete transaction of the transfer of possession of leased cargo containers in Tennessee, the court found that the sales tax did not impair uniformity or prevent the federal government from speaking with one voice when regulating commercial relations with foreign governments. The court held that the tax did not violate the Commerce Clause because there was a substantial nexus within Tennessee, the tax was fairly apportioned, did not discriminate and was fairly related to services by the state. Finally, the court determined that the sales tax, as applied to the cargo containers, did not violate the Import/Export, Supremacy or Due Process Clauses of the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of the commissioner and held that the imposition of the sales tax on the transfer of possession of the corporation's cargo containers was proper.

 

143 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Hanover v. Ruch


S/C No. 7


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


809 S.W.2d 893; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 156



April 15, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied May 20, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 199.


PRIOR HISTORY: From the Law Court, Shelby County, Hon. James E. Swearengen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant gynecologist sought review of the decision from the Court of Appeals (Tennessee), which affirmed the trial court's decision finding that the gynecologist committed the tort of criminal conversation and awarded the patient's husband compensatory and punitive damages. The gynecologist contended that the common-law tort of criminal conversation ought to be abolished in Tennessee.


OVERVIEW: The gynecologist and his patient had a sexual affair for a period of approximately two years. The patient's husband filed a divorce action against the patient to end their 31-year marriage and a civil action against the gynecologist. A jury found for the patient's husband on a claim of criminal conversation and assessed compensatory damages of $ 25,000 and punitive damages of $ 100,000. The lower court of appeals recommended the abolishment of the tort of criminal conversation but affirmed the trial court's decision. On appeal, the court reversed the lower appellate court's decision. The court held that: (1) the common law tort of criminal conversation was abolished by the state legislature on January 1, 1991, by 1990 Tenn. Pub. Acts 1056; (2) the court was not prohibited by the enactment from abolishing criminal conversation actions filed prior to January 1, 1991, where the exception for previously filed actions was merely a legislative recognition of the separation of legislative and judicial powers; and (3) where the reasons for the cause of action no longer existed, the common law tort of criminal conversation was abolished in Tennessee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's decision, which affirmed a judgment in favor of the patient's husband against the gynecologist for committing the tort of criminal conversation. The court abolished the common law tort of criminal conversation in Tennessee.

 

144 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Neal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


810 S.W.2d 131; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 137



April 8, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Withdrawn June 3, 1991. Reinstated By Order of the Court June 24, 1991.
Opinion withdrawn by State v. Neal, 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 202 (Tenn., June 3, 1991)


PRIOR HISTORY: From the Criminal Court, Davidson County, No. B-8867 Below, Hon. Raymond H. Leathers, Judge; C.C.A. No. 88-314-III; Appeal No. 01-S-01-9002-CR-00020.
Neal v. State, 1989 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 674 (Tenn. Crim. App., Sept. 22, 1989)



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS REVERSED; JUDGMENT OF DISMISSAL OF TRIAL COURT REINSTATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court dismissed defendant's petition for post-conviction relief after his guilty plea to the charge of attempting to pass a forged paper. The Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee) reversed the dismissal of defendant's petition, and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: When defendant entered his plea of guilty, the trial judge in the colloquy failed to inform defendant that the guilty plea could be used against defendant in a future habitual criminal proceeding and that a sentence for a future crime could be enhanced thereby. About 10 years later, defendant filed a petition for post-conviction relief, which the trial court dismissed. The lower appellate court reversed the dismissal, and the State appealed further. The court reversed the lower appellate court's action and reinstated the trial court's judgment of dismissal. The court held that the omission of which defendant complained was not required until 10 years after defendant entered his plea of guilty and that the omission did not represent a per se constitutional rights violation. The omission was not appropriately addressed in a post-conviction proceeding. The trial court substantially complied with the prescribed litany, and defendant's guilty plea was made knowingly and voluntarily. Although defendant was not expressly advised of all his constitutional rights, defendant testified at the post-conviction hearing that he knew those rights; therefore, there was no harm.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's reversal of the trial court's dismissal of defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. Defendant had pled guilty to attempting to pass a forged paper and challenged the constitutionality of the trial court's advice. The court held that the trial court's colloquy was in substantial compliance with the then prescribed litany and that defendant was not harmed.

 

145 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Lowe v. State


No. 192


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Knoxville


805 S.W.2d 368; 1991 Tenn. LEXIS 83



February 25, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Anderson County, The Hon. James B. Scott, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND DISMISSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Court of Criminal Appeals which granted petitioner's request for post-conviction relief and granted him an evidentiary hearing after determining that a jury instruction based on Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-1-804 was unconstitutional on the ground that it violated federal due process.


OVERVIEW: At petitioner's trial, the jury was instructed that a judgment of conviction of any person under the same name as that of petitioner was prima facie evidence that the identity of such person was the same as the petitioner. This language was taken, almost verbatim, from Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-1-804 (1982), governing the introduction of evidence of prior convictions in an habitual criminal prosecution. The Court of Appeals held that the instruction unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof on the issue of identity. On appeal, the court reversed. While the court agreed that the instruction violated due process, it was unnecessary to strike down the statute as unconstitutional per se. Also, petitioner was so clearly not entitled to relief, despite the constitutional error, that remand would amount to a futility. In order to state a "colorable claim," it was incumbent upon petitioner to allege that without the unconstitutional jury charge he would not have been found to be an habitual criminal. He not only failed to make such an allegation, but also effectively admitted in his pleadings that he had the requisite number of prior convictions to sustain his conviction as a recidivist.


OUTCOME: The judgment remanding petitioner's request for postconviction relief to the trial court was reversed, and the petition was dismissed for failure to state a claim upon which relief could be granted.

 

146 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Hart v. Johnson City


S/C No. 47


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Knoxville


801 S.W.2d 512; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 444



December 3, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Chancery Court, Washington County, Hon. Lynn W. Brown, Judge (By Interchange).



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners challenged the decision of the Chancery Court, Washington County (Tennessee), which upheld the constitutionality of a statute authorizing suits to contest municipal annexations and dismissed the owners' complaint against appellee city on the ground that the owners lacked standing to file a quo warranto action.


OVERVIEW: The city passed an ordinance annexing part of a community. The statute at issue permitted property owners adjacent to property annexed by municipal ordinance to bring a quo warranto action contesting the reasonableness of the annexation, but excluded certain adjacent owners based on population classifications. The owners insisted that the classifications were unconstitutional because they violated a state constitutional provision that the legislature provided the exclusive method by which municipal boundaries could be altered. The trial judge found facts that could reasonably be conceived to justify a classification between more populous and less populous counties, but failed to say what they were. The court reversed and found that the statute was unconstitutional. The court found that there was no evidence that the classifications were created according to any plan, rational or otherwise, but rather appeared to have been created at random and by default. The court held that the population classifications contained in the statute were prohibited by Tenn. Const. Art. XI, § 9.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the constitutionality of the statute authorizing suits to contest municipal annexations and dismissing the owners' complaint for lack of standing was reversed.

 

147 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Freeman v. Burson


No. 89-46-I


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


802 S.W.2d 210; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 337



October 1, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition to Rehear Denied December 7, 1992, Reported at 1992 Tenn. LEXIS 683.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Chancery Court, Davidson County, Hon. Irvin H. Kilcrease, Jr., Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed a judgment of the Chancery Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), which dismissed her action against defendant state that sought an injunction against enforcement of Tenn. Code Ann. § 2-7-111, an anti-electioneering statute, and a declaration that it was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The citizen brought an action for a permanent injunction against enforcement of Tenn. Code Ann. § 2-7-111, which prohibited the solicitation of votes and the display of campaign materials within a 100-foot radius of polling places on election day. She also sought a declaratory judgment that the statute was unconstitutional. The chancellor dismissed the action. On appeal, the court reversed. Section 2-7-111 was content-based because it regulated a specific subject matter and a certain category of speakers. Although the state showed a compelling interest in banning solicitation of voters or distribution of campaign materials within the polling place itself, § 2-7-111 was not narrowly tailored to advance the state's interest. The statute prohibited all campaign activity from an arc of 100 feet from every entrance to the polling places. In many instances this arc extended onto public streets and sidewalks. The state's interest in shielding voters from annoying campaign workers armed with cheap ball point pens and nail files could not justify an infringement upon free speech rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that dismissed the citizen's action to enjoin enforcement of an anti-electioneering statute and to have it declared unconstitutional. The court held that the statute was constitutionally invalid.

 

148 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boyd


No. 3


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


797 S.W.2d 589; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 324



September 24, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Criminal Court, Shelby County, Honorable Joseph B. McCartie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Criminal Court, Shelby County (Tennessee), which convicted him of felony murder and armed robbery and sentenced him to death for the felony murder conviction. The trial court imposed two life sentences for the armed robbery convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed a victim during a robbery. Defendant appealed his conviction for felony murder. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's convictions. Specifically, the evidence demonstrated that defendant announced his intent to kill his robbery victims if they resisted him and that, although the first round of ammunition could have been accidentally fired in the struggle, he shot the victim five times before he ran away. The court ruled that any possible error from the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury on the lesser offenses of manslaughter was harmless error. The sentence was not arbitrary, excessive, or disproportionate and did not violate the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments. The trial court properly instructed the jury by stating that the burden of proof was on the State to prove any statutory aggravating circumstances or circumstances beyond a reasonable doubt to a moral certainty. The court rejected defendant's argument that the trial court erred in instructing the jury to have no sympathy for defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for felony murder and armed robbery as well as his consecutive life sentences and his death sentence.

 

149 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lyons


S/C No. 1


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


802 S.W.2d 590; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 237



June 11, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Criminal Court, Dyer County, Hon. Joe G. Riley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for criminal trespass under former Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-3-1201(a) (Supp. 1988) and for trespass and disorderly conduct in a public school under former Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-3-1203 (Supp. 1988). The Criminal Court, Dyer County (Tennessee) dismissed the indictment on the ground that both statutes were unconstitutionally vague. The State directly appealed, pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 16-5-108(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested when he allegedly persisted in preaching and distributing religious literature on the grounds of a high school after the county school superintendent requested that he leave. The court determined that the statutes, which had been repealed and replaced, did not violate the Due Process Clause because of vagueness. The court determined that "lawful order" was an order having a legitimate basis and that, considering the nature and use of the subject property, its enforcement did not unlawfully inhibit or circumscribe defendant from engaging in constitutionally or statutorily protected conduct. The court added that it was the State's burden to prove that the order issued was lawful. The court explained that "trespass" was commonly understood to mean an entry upon the soil of another in the absence of lawful authority without the owner's license, and the element of disorderly conduct and its definition were intended to substitute for and expand upon the common-law requirement of a breach of the peace before there could have been criminal liability for an entry without license. The court added that its reasoning for "lawful order" applied to "lawful direction."


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holding of the trial court and remanded the case.

State v. Teel

Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death.

153 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Griffith


C.C.A. No. 6


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


787 S.W.2d 340; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 156



April 2, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Criminal Court, Obion County, Hon. Phil B. Harris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Criminal Court of Obion County (Tennessee), which resentenced him up to the maximum sentence provided for grand larceny, following revocation of his community corrections sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to grand larceny. He was sentenced to a three-year sentence, with 30 days to be served in the county jail and the remainder in a community correction program. He violated several conditions of the program and the trial court revoked his sentence and resentenced him to serve four years in the Department of Correction. He was given credit for time served in the county jail and the community correction program. Defendant claimed that the enlargement of his sentence violated the state and federal guarantees against double jeopardy. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that the sentencing of defendant to a community-based alternative to incarceration was not final and he had no legitimate expectation of finality in the severity of the sentence. Instead, he was placed on notice that upon revocation of the sentence due to his conduct that a greater sentence may be imposed. Accordingly, the decision to resentence defendant to a sentence greater than his original sentence did not subject him to multiple punishments for the same offense and there was no violation of the guarantees against double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which resentenced defendant to the maximum sentence provided for grand larceny, following revocation of his community corrections sentence.

State ex rel. Overton v. Taylor


No. 89-10-I

The State then requested that he be required to submit to a blood test, pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 24-7-112. 156 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Southern Living, Inc. v. Celauro


S/C No. 88-53-I


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


789 S.W.2d 251; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 120



March 5, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied Reported at: 789 S.W. 251 at 253.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Chancery Court, Davidson County, Hon. Irvin H. Kilcrease, Jr., Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED & REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review of a decision of the Chancery Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), which upheld defendant commissioner of revenue's treatment of the corporation's magazines for tax purposes as not having been eligible for an exemption that was available to newspapers and religious publications. The corporation had paid the sales and use taxes to the commissioner under protest.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner assessed a sales and/or use tax on the corporation's mail order subscription sales in the state pursuant to the Retailers Sales Tax Act, Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-6-201 et seq. The court reversed the finding against the corporation and held that the tax scheme was invalid as it affected the corporation and was in violation of their rights to freedom of speech and press under the First Amendment and the state constitution. The court disagreed with the commissioner's argument that newspapers' exemption from this tax under § 67-6-329(a)(3) furthered a vital public interest. The court stated that the immediate provision of news was not a compelling governmental interest and the exemption statute was not narrowly tailored to meet the asserted governmental interest. The tax that singled out the press, or that targeted individual publications within the press, placed a heavy burden on the state to justify its action and here the state failed to meet this heavy burden and had advanced no compelling justification for selective, content based taxation of the corporation's publications.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the commissioner's treatment of the corporation's publications for tax purposes as invalid and in violation of the corporation's right to free speech and press.

Newsweek, Inc. v. Celauro


S/C No. 88-54-I


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


789 S.W.2d 247; 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 121; 18 Media L. Rep. 1135



March 5, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 14, 1990, Reported at 1990 Tenn. LEXIS 205.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Chancery Court, Davidson County, Hon. Irvin H. Kilcrease, Jr., Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED & REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a decision of the Chancery Court, Davidson County (Tennessee), which affirmed a determination of defendant Commissioner of Revenue assessing a sales and/or use tax on the taxpayer's mail order subscription sales pursuant to the Retailers' Sales Tax Act, Tenn. Code Ann. § 67-6-201, et seq., in the taxpayer's action to recover sales taxes, penalty, and interest paid by the taxpayer under protest.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner assessed a sales tax on the taxpayer, a news magazine. The taxpayer paid the taxes and penalties assessed, but challenged the assessment as an unconstitutional violation of its First Amendment guarantees of freedom of the press and freedom of expression. The chancery court affirmed the Commissioner's determination, and the taxpayer sought review. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that in order for the state to impose a tax on the taxpayer's publication, which placed a burden on the taxpayer's First Amendment freedoms, there had to be a compelling governmental interest in distinguishing between the taxpayer's publication and newspapers, which were exempt. The court concluded that the immediate provision of news was not a compelling governmental interest, and that there was no basis for giving immediate news a privileged position over other avenues of news reporting. Moreover, the exemption statute was not narrowly tailored to meet the asserted governmental interest. The court found that there was no compelling justification for selective, content based taxation of the taxpayer's publication, and therefore, the tax was invalid under the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the chancery court's judgment in favor of the Commission and remanded for further proceedings.

 

158 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Underwood v. Liberty Mut. Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


782 S.W.2d 175; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 542



December 18, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Equity Court, Gibson County, Honorable Marion H. Holmes, Jr., Chancellor.



DISPOSITION:  MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED; CAUSE REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a decision of a chancellor (Tennessee) that awarded him workers' compensation benefits against appellee insurer. The employee contended that the award was inadequate.


OVERVIEW: The trial court concluded that the employee sustained compensable injury to both his back and his hip and awarded benefits. The employee contended on appeal that the award was inadequate. The insurer questioned causation of the hip injury. The court modified, affirmed, and remanded. The court noted that it was clear from a reading of the trial transcript and the depositions filed that there was material evidence to support the judgment of the trial court. The court noted that the trial court held that the insurance carrier would be liable for any future medical expenses and related costs incurred by the employee with respect to the herniated disc. The trial court declined, however, to award future medical expenses with regard to the employee's arthritic hip condition, despite finding that condition to be compensable. The court found that the trial court was in error finding that the employee was not entitled to recover any reasonable and necessary medical expenses in the future which were incurred as a result of a compensable injury. The court opined that it was premature for the trial court to attempt to limit or deny those benefits.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by finding that future expenses were recoverable and affirmed the award of workers' compensation benefits. The cause was remanded to the trial court for collection of costs accrued, for disbursement of the funds previously paid into court and for any further proceedings which might have been necessary.

 

159 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Prince


S/C Nos. 2, 4


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


781 S.W.2d 846; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 529



December 4, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the CRIMINAL Court, Shelby County, Hon. Joseph B. McCartie, Judge and Hon. W. Fred Axley, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, the State challenged a judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee), which reversed two trial courts' orders dismissing defendants' petitions for post-conviction relief as untimely, which held that defendants' petitions were not barred by the three-year statute of limitations set forth in Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-102, and which remanded the cases for further proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted under the Habitual Criminal Statute, Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-1-801. Defendants unsuccessfully appealed their convictions for the triggering felonies and the enhancement of their sentences as habitual criminals and raised unsuccessful post-conviction challenges to their convictions. Each then filed a post-conviction petition in which he attacked the validity of his prior guilty pleas. The trial courts denied defendants' petitions as, among other things, untimely. The court of criminal appeals reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the court of criminal appeals, holding that: (1) defendants did not waive their right to challenge their prior guilty pleas by having failed to include those challenges in prior post-conviction petitions attacking sentences enhanced by those guilty plea convictions; (2) defendants did not waive their right to vacate the enhanced sentences by having failed to establish any invalidity in the guilty pleas before filing their first petitions; and (3) defendants did not waive their right to rely on the invalidity of the underlying convictions by failing to attack the convictions in prior post-conviction proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of criminal appeals' decision in favor of defendants and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

160 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. McCormick


S. C. No. 87


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Knoxville


778 S.W.2d 48; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 454



September 25, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Criminal Court, Hamilton County, Hon. Joseph DiRisio, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Criminal Court, Hamilton County (Tennessee) for first degree murder as well as his capital sentence, claiming that there was insufficient proof to support the verdict or the aggravating factor found by the jury under Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-203(i)(6). He also contended that the introduction of his statements violated his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. On appeal he challenged the conviction and his sentence, claiming in part that the jury erred in finding the aggravating circumstances under § 39-2-203(i)(6) that he committed the murder to avoid, interfere with, or prevent his lawful arrest or prosecution. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, ruling that there was evidence from which the jury could have rationally concluded that he was guilty and that his intent was to prevent the victim from testifying against him in another matter. The court rejected the claim that the Sixth Amendment guarantee of counsel attached when defendant was only the focus of an investigation but had not yet been charged. The court also rejected his claim that § 39-2-203(i)(6) applied only to first-hand witnesses or to arrests or prosecutions that were underway. The statute was sufficiently clear to put a person of ordinary intelligence on notice of the homicide that was punishable by death. It was also sufficiently definite to guide the jury's discretion on what had to be found to impose a death sentence, and evidence supported the finding of the aggravating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. The court ordered that the sentence of death be carried out as provided by law unless otherwise ordered by the court or other proper authority.

 

161 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cauthern


S. Ct. No. 88-41-I


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Nashville


778 S.W.2d 39; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 451



September 25, 1989, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Patterson, 1989 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 866 (Tenn. Crim. App., Dec. 8, 1989)
Writ of certiorari denied Tennessee v. Cauthern, 495 U.S. 904, 110 S. Ct. 1922, 109 L. Ed. 2d 286, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 2116 (1990)
Appeal after remand at State v. Cauthern, 1996 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 747 (Tenn. Crim. App., Dec. 2, 1996)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Montgomery County, Hon. John H. Peay, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  REMANDED TO TRIAL COURT FOR RESENTENCING HEARING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Montgomery County trial court (Tennessee), which convicted him of felony murder, first-degree burglary, and aggravated rape. Co-defendant was also convicted, but his appeal was pending. Defendants were convicted after murdering a husband and his wife during a burglary, during which the wife was also raped. Defendant was sentenced to death, and co-defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: During the interrogation for the crimes with which defendant was charged, defendant sought to terminate the interrogation when he said that he was not guilty of rape, murder, or taken anything. Defendant tried to turn off the recording device, but he did not know that another device was kept running. Nonetheless, the officers did not terminate the interview at that time. The court found that the admissions of the contents of the statement made by defendant after that time constituted plain error in violation of defendant's Miranda rights. The statements related to items taken from the home that were later pawned or given to someone. The court further found that, although the admission of the statements was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt as to whether defendant was guilty of murder in the first degree, the error was not necessarily harmless when it came to defendant's death sentence. Therefore, a remand was required for resentencing. The court found no merit to defendant's argument that the death penalty statute was unconstitutional because it shifted the burden of proof to defendant to prove mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the verdict that imposed the death penalty upon defendant, and the action was remanded for a resentencing hearing. The court found no prejudicial error as to the conviction itself.

State v. Pilkey


No. 150


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Knoxville


776 S.W.2d 943; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 395



August 7, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the Criminal Court, McMinn County, Honorable R. Steve Bebb, Judge.



DISPOSITION: MODIFIED AND AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction, as affirmed by the Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee), for aggravated rape under Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-603(a)(4).


OVERVIEW: The state indicted defendant for the aggravated rape of his five-year-old nephew. A child protective services worker with the Department of Human Services videotaped an interview with the child. The child was not called as a witness by either party, but the videotaped interview was played before the jury. The jury found defendant guilty of aggravated rape, and the appellate court affirmed. The court found that the use of the videotaped statement was error and that Tenn. Code Ann. § 24-7-116(c)(1)(I)(ii) was unconstitutional as utilized in this case because it violated defendant's right to confrontation under the Sixth Amendment to the United States Constitution. However, the court affirmed the judgment of conviction because the evidence in the case, apart from the videotape, was so strong that the admission of the videotape into evidence was harmless error. The court held that the trial judge properly overruled defendant's motion to strike the introduction of the videotaped testimony because the child was never called as a witness for the purpose of giving evidence, and the trial judge impliedly determined the child's competency to testify.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for aggravated rape, as modified.

State v. Alley


No. 2


Supreme Court of Tennessee, at Jackson


776 S.W.2d 506; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 396



August 7, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From the CRIMINAL Court, Shelby County, Nos. 85-05085, 6, 7, Honorable W. Fred Axley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Criminal Court, Shelby County (Tennessee), which convicted him of premeditated first-degree murder, kidnapping, and aggravated rape. Defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of premeditated first-degree murder, kidnapping, and aggravated rape. The jury found that the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel. Defendant was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence. The court found that the state proved beyond a reasonable doubt defendant's sanity at the time of the offense. The expert testimony that defendant was not insane was strong and impressive. The court further found that the trial court did not err in granting the state's motion to exclude videotaped hypnotic and sodium amytal interviews of defendant from the jury's consideration. A jury of laypersons could not have understood and interpreted the hypnotic tapes in a reliable manner. Finally, the court found that the state did not commit prejudicial error in using learned treatises in cross-examining one of defendant's expert witnesses.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of premeditated first-degree murder, kidnapping, and aggravated rape was affirmed. The order sentencing defendant to death was affirmed.

Gold Watch, Inc. v. Memphis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


1989 Tenn. LEXIS 320



June 12, 1989, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] NOT FOR PUBLICATION


PRIOR HISTORY: Shelby Equity No. 1, No. 95560-2 R.D., George T. Lewis, Chancellor.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of an order entered in the Chancery Court of Shelby County (Tennessee) holding Memphis, Tenn., Ordinances No. 3647 constitutional. The corporation averred that the ordinance was unconstitutional as being vague and indefinite so as to violate the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was an establishment operating as a restaurant and lounge providing entertainment in the form of music and dancing waitresses. The corporation attempted to obtain a "Compensated Dance Permit," but the application was denied. The denial was predicated upon the violation of Memphis, Tenn. Ordinances No. 3647, § 20-123(A) (5), regarding the geographic limitation of operating a sexually oriented business within 1000 feet of any other sexually oriented business. The court agreed with the corporation that the ordinance in and of itself did not define what constituted "specified sexual activities" or "specified anatomical areas." However, upon reading the ordinance in its entirety, specific definitions were provided. The court held that the last paragraph of Memphis, Tenn., Ordinances No. 3647, § 20-123(E), which stated that a person, partnership, corporation, joint venture, or other business entity or organization opening after March 31, 1987, was subject to a maximum fine that was allowed by the city law, was penal and unconstitutional. The court ruled that upon deleting the unconstitutional portion there remained a complete law capable of enforcement.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by deleting the unconstitutional portion of the ordinance.

 

166 of 2007 DOCUMENTS


Franks v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


772 S.W.2d 428; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 261



June 5, 1989, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 12, 1989


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Williamson Chancery, Donald P. Harris, Judge, S. Ct. No. 88-28-I



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State sought review of a judgment of the Chancery Court of Williamson County (Tennessee), which upheld the additional compensation granted to plaintiff general session judge under § 20 of the Juvenile Court Restructure Act, codified at Tenn. Code Ann. § 37-1-210, for her services as a juvenile court judge.


OVERVIEW: The county board of commissioners approved a supplemental income for the judge as compensation for her additional duties as a juvenile court judge. Several years later, pursuant to audit, her additional appropriation was discontinued as unauthorized by state law. The trial court did not rule on the constitutionality of § 37-1-210. It disposed of the case by holding that any reduction in the judge's salary during her term of office violated Tenn. Const. art. VI, § 7. The court reversed the judgment granted to the judge. A juvenile judge was a judge of an inferior court within the meaning of Tenn. Const. art VI, § 7, and compensation for her services was to be "ascertained by law." The law ascertaining the amount of compensation had to be enacted by the legislature, the only law-making power, and its power was not allowed to be delegated to any other body. Thus, the statement in Tenn. Code Ann. § 37-1-210 that permitted counties to provide additional compensation to general sessions judges who exercised juvenile court jurisdiction was unconstitutional. The act was voidable until condemned by judicial pronouncement. Therefore, the excess monies paid and received were not recoverable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Thompson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


768 S.W.2d 239; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 47



February 27, 1989, Filed

Writ of certiorari denied Thompson v. Tennessee, 497 U.S. 1031, 111 L. Ed. 2d 796, 110 S. Ct. 3288, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 3596, 58 U.S.L.W. 3835 (1990)
Post-conviction relief denied at Thompson v. State, 958 S.W.2d 156, 1997 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 522 (Tenn. Crim. App., 1997)

DISPOSITION:  AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Coffee Criminal Court (Tennessee), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and a minor female kidnapped a woman in a store's parking lot, and defendant killed her with a knife. Defendant was later given a speeding ticket while driving the victim's car. Defendant and the minor were arrested the following day. While in custody, defendant gave a statement admitting that he killed the victim. Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree murder and defendant's death sentence.

Secretary of State v. St. Augustine Church/St. Augustine School


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


766 S.W.2d 499; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 48



February 27, 1989, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Davidson Equity, Honorable Irvin H. Kilcrease, Jr. Chancellor



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor non-profit organizations challenged a decision of the Davidson Equity Court (Tennessee), which found for plaintiff Secretary of State. The chancery court held Tenn. Code Ann § 39-6-609 unconstitutional under the provisions of Tenn. Const. art. XI, § 5 because § 39-6-609 exempted bingo from the general gambling laws and legalized its play as applied to various charitable, religious, fraternal, and other non-profit organizations.


OVERVIEW: The chancery court found § 39-6-609 unconstitutional under art. XI, § 5, which prohibited lotteries within the state. Section 39-6-609 had exempted bingo from the general gambling laws and regulated its conduct by a licensing system. The general assembly had removed bingo from the definition of a lottery by terming the consideration paid by a customer as a charitable contribution rather than a wager. The non-profit organizations, which held bingo permits, argued that art. XI. § 5 was designed to abolish and outlaw only the older forms of "ticket lotteries" which were prevalent in the early nineteenth century. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that bingo clearly constituted a lottery within the meaning of art. XI, § 5, which provided a "blanket" prohibition against lotteries in all forms. The game of bingo, when conducted on a commercial scale for profit with chances or tickets sold for consideration, was a form of gambling and fell under the generally accepted definition of the term "lottery."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the chancery court's decision in favor of the Secretary of State, holding unconstitutional certain statutes exempting bingo from Tennessee's general gambling laws as applied to various charitable, religious, fraternal, and other non-profit organizations. The court remanded the cause to the chancery court for collection of costs and for any further proceedings.

State v. West


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


767 S.W.2d 387; 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 28



February 6, 1989, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition to Rehear Denied March 27, 1989.
Writ of certiorari denied West v. Tennessee, 497 U.S. 1010, 110 S. Ct. 3254, 111 L. Ed. 2d 764, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 3432, 58 U.S.L.W. 3818 (1990)
Post-conviction relief denied at West v. State, 1998 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 636 (Tenn. Crim. App., June 12, 1998)
Subsequent civil proceeding at, Complaint dismissed at West v. Ray, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 101834 (M.D. Tenn., Sept. 24, 2010)
Subsequent civil proceeding at, Remanded by West v. Ray, 2010 Tenn. LEXIS 1072 (Tenn., Nov. 6, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Union Criminal, SC # 3, Honorable Lee Asbury, Judge
State v. West, 728 S.W.2d 32, 1986 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 2555 (Tenn. Crim. App., 1986)



DISPOSITION:  GUILT AFFIRMED; DEATH SENTENCE AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Union County Trial Court (Tennessee), which convicted defendant of two counts of first degree premeditated murder, aggravated kidnapping, and aggravated rape. Defendant was sentenced to death for each murder and to 40 years imprisonment on each of the convictions for rape and kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: The court held that defendant had the burden of showing that the evidence preponderated against the ruling. The court affirmed defendant's convictions, holding that there was ample evidence to support the trial judge's ruling. The court held that it carefully examined the record relevant to the numerous alleged instances of improper cross examination and found that no prejudicial error occurred during the cross examination of defendant. The court concluded that the trial court correctly denied defendant's motion to change venue, holding that a reading of the voir dire revealed that the questioning about any bias or prejudice based upon publicity or community comment was carefully explored and the trial judge's participation was clarifying rather than rehabilitative. The court held that the prosecutor's improper statements minimized the jury's role and allowed them to feel that the responsibility for a death sentence rested elsewhere. However, the court held that because of the trial judge's clarifying measures, the errors were harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The court affirmed the sentence of death, holding that it was neither excessive nor disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for two counts of first degree premeditated murder, aggravated kidnapping, and aggravated rape. The court affirmed the death sentence.

State v. Sutton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Tennessee


761 S.W.2d 763; 1988 Tenn. LEXIS 192



October 24, 1988, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied December 5, 1988, Reported at: 1988 Tenn. LEXIS 264.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] MORGAN CRIMINAL, Hon. E. Eugene Eblen, Judge. S/C No. 3



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Morgan County Criminal Court (Tennessee), which convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder after the testimony of four fellow inmates linked him to the brutal stabbing death of another inmate. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence. The court determined that the evidence against defendant was sufficient to support the first-degree murder conviction beyond a reasonable doubt. The court noted that while the four inmates might not have been ideal witnesses, any inconsistencies or contradictions in their testimony were not so great that their testimony had to be discounted as a matter of law. The court found that defendant's appearing at his arraignment without an attorney did not result in any prejudice to him. With respect to the death sentence, the court found that: 1) it was not imposed in an arbitrary fashion; 2) the evidence supported the jury's findings of three statutory aggravating circumstances and an absence of any mitigating circumstances that would outweigh the aggravating circumstances; and 3) the sentence of death was neither excessive or disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases, considering both the nature of the crime and the defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of first degree murder and the sentence of death imposed by the jury.
